Concurrency Problems ^a11GgUfU

The Lost UPDATE Problem ^xaT8xZcQ

The Temporary Update (or Dirty Read) 
Problem ^NRAVURaw

The Unrepeatable Read Problem ^IJME5UiZ

one transaction’s update gets overwritten by another transaction, like it never happened. ^rGZcvRrf

ex: ^37qBhG3I

It’s called              conflict because both transactions write the same item X, and one write kills the other. ^LrPqrtwb

write-write ^hm5VdbTm

T2 reads data written by T1 before T1 commits.
Then T1 aborts (rolls back), so T2 used a value that was never supposed to be real. ^dhLhlDog

the same SELECT gives different results inside the same transaction, because someone else committed an update in between. the "t3" ^jVAZlQrq

This happens when you compute an aggregate (like SUM) while updates are happening, so you mix “before” and “after” values and get a wrong total. ^qkem0PVq

“Phantom” means: you run the same query twice and you get extra rows (new rows appear) because another transaction inserted (or deleted) rows that match your condition. ^lzXFOjCg

ex: ^mlFuauzG

It’s                 conflict because:
One transaction writes,
another transaction reads that uncommitted write. ^mrrz5u6B

write-read ^KYPJ90RM

It’s                conflict because:
First transaction reads,
another transaction writes before the first one reads again. ^lSidazSt

read-write ^dQxDs07I

The Incorrect Summary Problem ^dJAKd9zR

The Phantom Record Problem ^XlEkrWU6

ex: ^RL7ILrrm

ex: ^uWXNm0X7

Properties of Transactions (ACID) ^qUcAyTyP

A transaction is not “just SQL”. It must behave in a safe way, so the database stays correct even with many users + crashes. These safety rules are called : ACID ^cVeFIoeP

Atomic ^QVwkWw6M

Consistent ^TxLuymiJ

Isolated ^hkZzkLxD

Durable ^3uJ88pjG

Atomicity means no half-transaction. If it fails anywhere, DB cancels everything like it never started. ^p2TZNzOh

Example: 

Transfer: subtract from A and  add to B . 
If crash after subtracting, DB must undo subtract. ^FWnvDIg5

means rules of real life must stay true after the transaction finishes. The DBMS helps, but the rules come from your design/business logic. ^h2Xd4mmJ

means: even if 100 people run transactions at once, each one should feel like:

“I ran alone. Nobody messed with my steps.” 

This is exactly what prevents lost update, dirty read, incorrect summary, phantom, etc ^8f7RarYl

means: after you COMMIT, even if power goes off, the change must still be there when DB restarts. ^bywxR7jf

Example: Two people buying the last seat: isolation stops the “double sell”. ^dVpRKnzY

DBMS uses logging + writing to persistent storage. ^dFtjTd7R

Example: You paid and it says “success”. If the server crashes right after, your payment record must not vanish. ^Rl5hTWny

Serializability (serial vs serializable schedules) ^W1ENaeXm

Transaction Isolation Levels ^91LhqGxG

2- Serializable = DB may mix steps (faster), BUT the final result must be the same as some serial order.
Meaning: even if operations interleave, the outcome must match either (T1 then T2) or (T2 then T1). ^4ZUdM3Gs

1- Serial = DB runs transactions one by one, no mixing.
So T1 finishes completely, then T2 starts. This is always safe, but slow. ^qfz2GrxO

NOTE : Schedule = the “timeline” of what happened: read/write actions from T1, T2, T3… in the order the DB executed them.

3- A serial schedule : is one in which transactions are executed one after the other.

If that timeline shows T1 fully, then T2 fully → that schedule is serial. ^WlN1ACVN

4- Nonserial means transactions are mixed like:
T1 does 1 step → T2 does 1 step → T1 does next step → … ^cJZumPhX

IN SHORT : ^AngkfUps

The goal of concurrency control is to allow concurrent execution of transactions while preserving serial equivalence. ^P5t3lTxy

Isolation level = how strict the DB is about hiding other people’s unfinished work from you.
 ^1qESESD0

More strict = fewer problems, but slower/more locking.
Less strict = faster, but you may see weird things (dirty reads, phantoms, etc.). ^urJ3p7qm

Read Uncommitted ^n00DQa3r

Read Committed ^maDKXmok

Repeatable Read ^Jvu2WYmb

Serializable ^TyO4PEuw

Snapshot ^ALYgXd52

Snapshot isolation means: when your transaction starts, the DB gives you a frozen picture (photo) of the database. During this transaction, you keep reading from that photo, so your data does not change while you are working. ^XV36nutd

This prevents dirty, unrepeatable, and phantom reads inside one transaction.
However, if you run the same transaction again later, results may differ because a new snapshot is taken after the database has changed. ^sopgRVLL

data can change during the transaction execution: nonrepeatable read and phantom records ^fn9ML4LC

(so no dirty reads). ^EY0Tq0xw

Assume bal = 150 before Alice and Bob start their transactions ^cTIZerLs

ex: ^mZqM71FE

1- You do SELECT balance and see 150.
2- Another transaction commits balance = 120.
3- You run the same SELECT balance again inside your transaction     
    and now see 120 → this is nonrepeatable read. ^9veYN0pJ

Concurrency Control Approaches ^MMMbo8jg

Pessimistic CC: 
   assumes conflicts happen frequently 
→ transactions must explicitly require locks ^ibWvQiIf

Optimistic CC: 
assumes conflicts are rare → avoids locking → checks conflicts at commit → if conflict, abort ^LprJb7bz

 ^I4ZGLnSm

Secondary index = dense + unclustered ^Fqqy1Coo

some calc: ^3oZuzxHr

data will be used in the calc: ^DDQFaLM1

# Primary index is SPARSE
# One index entry per DATA BLOCK

ri = number of index entries
ri = number of data blocks
ri = 3000

# Size of one index entry
Ri = key + pointer
Ri = 9 + 6 = 15 bytes

# Blocking factor (entries per index block)
bfri = floor(B / Ri)
bfri = floor(1024 / 15)
bfri = 68

# Number of index blocks
bi = ceil(ri / bfri)
bi = ceil(3000 / 68)
bi = 45

# Binary search on index
index_access = ceil(log2(45)) = 6

# Access data block
data_access = 1

# Total cost
total_access = 6 + 1 = 7 block accesses ^ivgvc1hy

# Clustering index is ALSO SPARSE
# One entry per DISTINCT VALUE 
or BLOCK GROUP

# In practice, calculation is SAME STYLE 
as primary index

ri = number of data blocks (or groups)
ri ≈ 3000

Ri = 15 bytes
bfri = 68

bi = ceil(3000 / 68)
bi = 45

index_access = ceil(log2(45)) = 6
data_access  = 1

total_access = 6 + 1 = 7 block accesses
 ^BD6XXJx7

PRIMARY INDEX CALCULATION ^ps0EZ4Z6

CLUSTERING INDEX CALCULATION ^Wq0NsT85

# Secondary index is DENSE
# One index entry per RECORD

ri = number of records
ri = 30000

Ri = 15 bytes
bfri = 68

bi = ceil(30000 / 68)
bi = 442

# Binary search on index
index_access = ceil(log2(442)) = 9

# Access actual record
data_access = 1

total_access = 9 + 1 = 10 block accesses
 ^uvmHvnIy

MULTI_LEVEL INDEX:  ^7IJP4da6

Construct a second-level index on a first-level index
Continue until the top level fits in one block ^E2EUgmnz

important: ^bdyZV4L9

3- ^nkH5icmg

before with the single-Level (second.index) ^3tjCadIV

now with the multi-lv-secondary index ^DiBdh4ak

# Given
ri = 30000      # records (secondary index → dense)
bf = 68              # blocking factor (fan-out)

# Level 1 (index on data)
b1 = ceil(30000 / 68)
b1 = 442 blocks

# Level 2 (index on level 1)
b2 = ceil(442 / 68)
b2 = 7 blocks

# Level 3 (index on level 2)
b3 = ceil(7 / 68)
b3 = 1 block

# Number of index levels
t = 3

# Total search cost
index_access = t = 3
data_access  = 1

total_access = 3 + 1 = 4 block accesses
 ^wyNU162Y

Huge improvement. ^MpkwcBra

Because each index level reduces the search space by the fan-out, leading to logarithmic access time. ^ZNlCFGdQ

Tree-Structured Indexes (B-Tree, B+-Tree) ^qzvUvI5a

Dynamic Tree Structured (Multi-level) Indexes ^Pc8OSc9M

It:
Grows when data grows
Shrinks when data shrinks
Always stays balanced ^uWlOxrbG

its  the enhanced version or diff version
of B-tree, 
• All data pointers are in LEAF nodes only
• Internal nodes store ONLY keys
• Leaf nodes are linked (linked list)
• Keys appear in internal AND leaf nodes (duplicated)  ^Q7Ym47Pc

as a summary : ^6EU9Mn2s

Index-organized tables in Oracle ^1oy5uUwV

in some DB systems like oracle not just the key stored as an index , also the data
stored in the with the index in the same way : ^tZm1zRQy

4-Hash Indexes ^zGNbb140

very important : ^zeYZKS1Z

Index Design ^wi9wSde7

1. Index(ID)
2. Index(fName)
3. Index(lName)
4. Index(fName, lName)
5. Index(lName, fName)
6. Index(ID, fName)
7. Index(ID, lName) ^ZouDictb

so we can have alot of combinations,
but this is not we want  ^bPE4A8gx

note: ^DyVAE44j

so we have a lot of cases we can create  the index with :
so the rules are : ^Za4qS0t4

• Index design depends on workload
• Index columns used in WHERE and JOIN
• Avoid too many indexes on write-heavy tables
• Composite indexes can speed up multiple queries
• DBMS uses only ONE index per query ^5n6oK5B8

lets check some problems: ^w3S6lQDO

this is the realtion we have :    V(M, N, P) ^erEzdLsv

ex problem 1 ^hSJvn383

V → the table (relation) name

M, N, P → the attributes (columns) of the table ^nLKfSy1x

Which indexes should be created? ^l0EZpyfN

1- Index1 on V(N) and
2-Index2 on V(P)
Can be any type (B-tree or Hash) ^kkCT8Slr

but if we asked Which indexes should be
 created for best performance? ^49pNRlue

Index on V(N) only
Ignore P due to low frequency ^4YAYeXg5

note : since it is Equality queries only
→ Hash OK
→ B-tree OK
→ Type does not matter ^cozn1ivZ

ex problem 2 ^xgQ91FGg

Which indexes should be created? ^2ihkvVgd

1- Index1 on V(N)
2- Index2 on V(P)
• Index on N → MUST be B-tree
• Index on P → Can be B-tree or Hash ^Q2I84Sv1

but if we asked Which indexes should be
 created for best performance? ^WJL5fote

1- Index on V(N) ONLY
2- Ignore P since it has low freq.  ^jOlHLI98

• Query on N is a RANGE query (>, <)
  → Hash index NOT allowed
  → B-tree REQUIRED

• Query on P is equality only (=)
  → Hash OK
  → B-tree OK ^lpfqWFBT

ex problem 3 ^Ynm1iMHa

Which indexes should be created? ^yVMgJaF9

for "WHERE N = ? AND P > ?" if we choose index(N) and index(P)
seperatly DBMS will choose one:
Either index on N
OR index on P
Then it must filter the other condition manually. which is :  Inefficient.

instead :

1-Index(N, P)
Uses both conditions together
No extra filtering
Much fewer disk accesses


 ^EuJVXPyx

=> B-tree ^5Li4nXOd

Index design should prioritize frequent queries, avoid indexing for rare queries in write-heavy workloads, focus on WHERE clauses, prefer composite indexes that support multiple queries, and remember that the DBMS uses only one index per query. ^jImWLTT9

in short : ^r7iJabY4
